```latex
\section{Introduction}

Twitter has become an integral platform for political communication processes, particularly among members of the U.S. Congress
\cite{Aragn2013CommunicationDI, Huberman2008SocialNT, Carlisle2013IsSM, Theocharis2020TheDO, Hemphill2013WhatsCD, Straus2013CommunicatingI1}. Through
Twitter, Congress members disseminate political information, engage in discussions, and interact with fellow members and constituents. While previous
work has investigated patterns of incivility, polarization, and campaign strategies on Twitter \cite{Theocharis2020TheDO, Garca2015IdeologicalAT,
Hua2020CharacterizingTU, Bail2019AssessingTR}, less is known about the specific dynamics and factors influencing interactions among members of the
U.S. Congress. This critical gap in our understanding of online political discourse dynamics calls for in-depth investigation beyond party lines to
include factors such as Represented State and Chamber.

This study addresses this research gap by analyzing the Twitter interactions among members of the 117th U.S. Congress over a four-month period. Our
data comprise a directed graph of social interactions among Congress members, each characterized by their Represented State, Political Party, and
Chamber \cite{Theocharis2020TheDO, Hua2020CharacterizingTU, Badawy2018WhoFF, Glassman2013SocialNA}. Expanding upon prior studies that explored the
role of party lines on Twitter interactions \cite{Chamberlain2021ANA, Valle2021PoliticalIB, Aragn2013CommunicationDI, Theocharis2020TheDO,
Hua2020CharacterizingTU}, our study provides a more holistic understanding by examining the interactions across attributes of Party, Chamber, and size
of States represented.

To investigate these three factors' influence on Twitter interactions, we employed a mixture of descriptive statistics and regression analysis
\cite{Perry2018EgocentricNA, Benton2016LearningME, Himelboim2017ClassifyingTT}. This methodological framework allows us to identify key patterns of
Twitter engagements and construct a robust model capturing the influences of Party, Chamber, and State sizes on these interactions. By analyzing the
influence of these factors, we provide unique insights into how various aspects of political representation shape online engagement.

Our analysis reveals nuanced differences in Twitter engagement across Parties and Chambers of Congress members. Furthermore, our findings attest to
the significant role of State representation size in influencing the level of Twitter interactions among Congress members \cite{Kwak2010WhatIT}. These
insights pave the way for a more sophisticated understanding of the intricate dynamics of online political discourse among U.S. Congress members,
raising further questions on the interplay between these factors and their impact on broader political communication dynamics. This richly layered
analysis provides a foundation upon which to explore additional factors influencing Congress members' Twitter interactions, underscoring this study's
relevance for future research in the field.
```

[12] ---- USER {Writer} -> discussion <background_paper_sections:introduction>  ----------------------------------------------------------------------
Here is the Introduction section of the paper:

\section{Introduction}

Twitter has become an integral platform for political communication processes, particularly among members of the U.S. Congress
\cite{Aragn2013CommunicationDI, Huberman2008SocialNT, Carlisle2013IsSM, Theocharis2020TheDO, Hemphill2013WhatsCD, Straus2013CommunicatingI1}. Through
Twitter, Congress members disseminate political information, engage in discussions, and interact with fellow members and constituents. While previous
work has investigated patterns of incivility, polarization, and campaign strategies on Twitter \cite{Theocharis2020TheDO, Garca2015IdeologicalAT,
Hua2020CharacterizingTU, Bail2019AssessingTR}, less is known about the specific dynamics and factors influencing interactions among members of the
U.S. Congress. This critical gap in our understanding of online political discourse dynamics calls for in-depth investigation beyond party lines to
include factors such as Represented State and Chamber.

This study addresses this research gap by analyzing the Twitter interactions among members of the 117th U.S. Congress over a four-month period. Our
data comprise a directed graph of social interactions among Congress members, each characterized by their Represented State, Political Party, and
Chamber \cite{Theocharis2020TheDO, Hua2020CharacterizingTU, Badawy2018WhoFF, Glassman2013SocialNA}. Expanding upon prior studies that explored the
role of party lines on Twitter interactions \cite{Chamberlain2021ANA, Valle2021PoliticalIB, Aragn2013CommunicationDI, Theocharis2020TheDO,
Hua2020CharacterizingTU}, our study provides a more holistic understanding by examining the interactions across attributes of Party, Chamber, and size
of States represented.

To investigate these three factors' influence on Twitter interactions, we employed a mixture of descriptive statistics and regression analysis
\cite{Perry2018EgocentricNA, Benton2016LearningME, Himelboim2017ClassifyingTT}. This methodological framework allows us to identify key patterns of
Twitter engagements and construct a robust model capturing the influences of Party, Chamber, and State sizes on these interactions. By analyzing the
influence of these factors, we provide unique insights into how various aspects of political representation shape online engagement.

Our analysis reveals nuanced differences in Twitter engagement across Parties and Chambers of Congress members. Furthermore, our findings attest to
the significant role of State representation size in influencing the level of Twitter interactions among Congress members \cite{Kwak2010WhatIT}. These
insights pave the way for a more sophisticated understanding of the intricate dynamics of online political discourse among U.S. Congress members,
raising further questions on the interplay between these factors and their impact on broader political communication dynamics. This richly layered
analysis provides a foundation upon which to explore additional factors influencing Congress members' Twitter interactions, underscoring this study's
relevance for future research in the field.

```latex
\section{Discussion}

This study focused on the examination and quantification of Twitter interactions among members of the 117th US Congress. In recognizing Twitter's
growing influence as a platform for political communication, this work builds on prior research that has investigated patterns of incivility,
polarization, campaign strategies, and factional behaviors on Twitter \cite{Aragn2013CommunicationDI, Huberman2008SocialNT, Carlisle2013IsSM,
Theocharis2020TheDO, Hemphill2013WhatsCD, Straus2013CommunicatingI1, Garca2015IdeologicalAT, Hua2020CharacterizingTU, Bail2019AssessingTR,
Chamberlain2021ANA, Valle2021PoliticalIB}.

Through a comprehensive analysis of interactions over a four-month period, we uncovered patterns and relationships by interpreting the social network
through directed graph representation and considering three attributes: Represented State, Political Party, and Chamber. Our methodological framework,
which significantly relied on statistical techniques, yielded insights that indicate nuanced differences in Twitter engagement across parties and
chambers and the significant role of State representation size in influencing interaction levels \cite{Theocharis2020TheDO, Hua2020CharacterizingTU,
Badawy2018WhoFF, Glassman2013SocialNA}.

Comparing our results with prior research, our finding about the influence of State representation size seems to be novel. Prior studies, such as that
by Chamberlain (2021) \cite{Chamberlain2021ANA} and Valle (2021) \cite{Valle2021PoliticalIB}, primarily focused on partisan behaviors and factional
activities among Congress members on Twitter.

There are, however, limitations to consider in this study. First, our Twitter data covers only a period of four months, possibly missing nuances from
interactions over longer periods. Additionally, the study does not consider the content of the tweets, retweets, replies, or the context within which
interactions occurred. Also, the exclusion of Congress members with fewer than 100 tweets during the data collection period may have unintentionally
excluded some active but less frequent Twitter users from the analysis.

In conclusion, our work brings forth nuanced insights into the Twitter interactions among members of the US Congress, showcasing the role of State
representation size, in addition to the Party and Chamber. Our findings provide valuable constructs for further studying political communication
dynamics. Future investigations could consider a longer investigation period or incorporate the content or context of Twitter interactions to gain
richer insights into the political discourse. Additionally, studies expanding to other social media platforms might offer a broader view of online
political discourse dynamics among legislators.
```

```latex
\section{Discussion}

This study embarked on a deep exploration aimed at understanding the dynamics of Twitter interactions among the members of the 117th US Congress.
Building upon previous studies \cite{Aragn2013CommunicationDI, Theocharis2020TheDO, Himelboim2013WhatsCD}, we extend our comprehension of political
discourse dynamics by exploring beyond party and chamber lines. We incorporate the role of represented state size, a facet relatively less explored in
previous investigation \cite{Chamberlain2021ANA, Valle2021PoliticalIB}.

Our methodology involved constructing a directed graph representing Twitter interactions among Congress members over four months, which were then
analyzed through a mixture of descriptive statistics and regression models. Contrary to preceding studies that mainly reported on party-centric
engagements \cite{Aragn2013CommunicationDI, Chamberlain2021ANA, Valle2021PoliticalIB}, the principal finding of our study revolves around the
influence of state representation size. We discovered a substantial increase in Twitter interactions with the augmentation of state representation
size, pushing beyond the prevalent narrative of party polarization.

Nonetheless, certain limitations of the study should be recognized. Firstly, our analysis was based on a relatively short timeframe of four months,
which may not accurately represent the nuances of interactions over an extended period. Secondly, we do not distinguish between different types of
engagements, such as 'likes', 'retweets', or 'replies', nor do we explore the context or content of these interactions. Accordingly, our methodology
may miss nuances related to the quality and content of the interactions. Also, setting a minimum tweet threshold for inclusion may lead to an
unintended exclusion of active members who use Twitter more sparingly.

Our work holds implications for the broader understanding of political dynamics within Congress. The significant impact of the size of state
representation on Twitter interactions suggests that geographical representation and related local interests may inject a complex layer to political
discourse, which can in turn affect policy-making and consensus building. It calls for stakeholders to consider these subtleties when exploring
strategies for better political communication, especially in the current age marked by digital transformations and polarized politics.

Looking ahead, several intriguing pathways for future research have emerged from our study. An exploration into the content or sentiment analysis of
Twitter interactions can unravel the depth of these online political discourses. Future studies might also consider including a variety of social
media platforms to enrich understanding of these interactions \cite{Hemphill2013WhatsCD}. Moreover, integrating the insights from our study with
research into online political vernacular \cite{Rathje2021OutgroupAD} could illuminate how language usage varies with the size of the state
representation.

In conclusion, our investigation unveils the intricate dynamics of the online political discourse among the members of the US Congress by focusing on
represented state size, in addition to party and chamber. Hence, paving the way for more nuanced insights into political communication and hinting at
the fruitful avenues for future research.
```

```latex
\section{Discussion}

This study embarked on a deep exploration aimed at understanding the dynamics of Twitter interactions among the members of the 117th US Congress.
Adding to existing knowledge of political communication on Twitter \cite{Aragn2013CommunicationDI, Theocharis2020TheDO, Hemphill2013WhatsCD,
Straus2013CommunicatingI1}, we sought to offer more holistic insights by incorporating the nuanced influences of not just Party and Chamber, but also
the size of the Represented State - a facet relatively less examined \cite{Chamberlain2021ANA, Valle2021PoliticalIB}.

Our methodology entailed constructing a directed graph representing Twitter interactions among Congress members over four months, which were then
analysed using a blend of descriptive statistics and regression models. Unlike preceding studies that have largely focused on party-centric
engagements \cite{Aragn2013CommunicationDI, Chamberlain2021ANA, Valle2021PoliticalIB}, our principal finding has been the significant role played by
the size of state representation.

While the conclusions drawn add value to our understanding of political dynamics on Twitter, there are certain limitations. The data spanned only four
months, potentially overlooking the intricacies of interactions over the long-term. Further, the analysis failed to differentiate between the types of
engagements (such as 'likes', 'retweets', or 'replies'), and ignored the context or content of these interactions. Also, the minimum tweet threshold
set for inclusion in the database may have inadvertently excluded less frequent, yet active, Twitter users from the sample.

Our findings have implications for the broader understanding of political dynamics within Congress. The revelation that attributes beyond party lines,
such as a state’s representation size, can influence Twitter interactions - thereby indirectly impacting information dissipation and nurturing of
political ideas – advocates for a reconsideration of the current understanding of political discourses \cite{Hemphill2013WhatsCD}. It underscores the
need for inclusivity of these subtleties when planning social media strategies for political communication, particularly in our current age
characterized by digital transformation and polarized political landscapes.

It paves the way for several fascinating avenues for prospective research. Diving into a qualitative analysis of Twitter interactions, including
aspects such as sentiment and content, could unearth valuable details about these political discourses. Exploring a diverse range of social media
platforms apart from Twitter could offer a broader perspective on these interactions \cite{Borge-Holthoefer2014ContentAN}. Furthermore, integrating
the insights drawn from our study with research focusing on the tone and tenor of online political conversations \cite{Rathje2021OutgroupAD} might
throw light on how language constructs vary with factors such as the size of state representation, and thereby aid in the development of a
comprehensive understanding of online political discourses.

In summary, this study has helped demystify some of the nuances in online political discourse among US Congress members. It has underscored the
sensitivity of these discourses to factors beyond party-lines and chambers, such as State representation size, thus broadening the purview of
political communication studies, and indicating promising paths for future investigations.
```
